<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Clothing</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Object that covers a portion of the body</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Outfit" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Outfit_(disambiguation)" title="Outfit (disambiguation)">Outfit (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Garment" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Garment_(disambiguation)" title="Garment (disambiguation)">Garment (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clothes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2460" data-file-width="1566" decoding="async" height="346" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Clothes.jpg/220px-Clothes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Clothes.jpg/330px-Clothes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Clothes.jpg/440px-Clothes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clothes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/History_of_clothing_and_textiles" title="History of clothing and textiles">Clothing in history</a>, showing (from top) Egyptians, Ancient Greeks, Romans; Byzantines, Franks; and thirteenth through fifteenth century Europeans</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KangaSiyu1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="1704" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/KangaSiyu1.jpg/220px-KangaSiyu1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/KangaSiyu1.jpg/330px-KangaSiyu1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/KangaSiyu1.jpg/440px-KangaSiyu1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KangaSiyu1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanga_(African_garment)" title="Kanga (African garment)">kanga</a>, worn throughout the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region</div></div></div>
<p><b>Clothing</b> (also known as <b>clothes</b>, <b>apparel</b>, and <b>attire</b>) are items worn on the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">body</a>. Typically, clothing is made of fabrics or <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textiles</a>, but over time it has included garments made from animal skin and other thin sheets of materials and natural products found in the environment, put together. The wearing of clothing is mostly restricted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_being" title="Human being">human beings</a> and is a feature of all human societies. The amount and type of clothing worn depends on gender, body type, social factors, and geographic considerations. Garments cover the body, <a href="/wiki/Footwear" title="Footwear">footwear</a> covers the feet, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gloves" title="Gloves">gloves</a> cover the hands, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hats" title="Hats">hats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Headgear" title="Headgear">headgear</a> cover the head. <a href="/wiki/Eyewear" title="Eyewear">Eyewear</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewelry" title="Jewelry">jewelry</a> are not generally considered items of clothing, but play an important role in <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">fashion</a> and clothing as <a href="/wiki/Costume" title="Costume">costume</a>.
</p><p>Clothing serves many purposes: it can serve as protection from the elements, rough surfaces, sharp stones, rash-causing plants, insect bites, by providing a barrier between the skin and the environment. Clothing can insulate against cold or hot conditions, and it can provide a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygienic" title="Hygienic">hygienic</a> barrier, keeping infectious and toxic materials away from the body. It can protect feet from injury and discomfort or facilitate navigation in varied environments. Clothing also provides protection from <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet radiation</a>. It  may be used to prevent glare or increase visual acuity in harsh environments, such as brimmed hats. Clothing is used for protection against injury in specific tasks and occupations, sports, and warfare. Fashioned with pockets, belts, or loops, clothing may provide a means to carry things while freeing the hands.
</p><p>Clothing has significant social factors as well. Wearing clothes is a variable <a href="/wiki/Dress_code" title="Dress code">social norm</a>. It may connote <a href="/wiki/Modesty" title="Modesty">modesty</a>. Being deprived of clothing in front of others may be <a href="/wiki/Embarrassment" title="Embarrassment">embarrassing</a>. In many parts of the world, not wearing clothes in public so that <a href="/wiki/Sex_organ#Mammals" title="Sex organ">genitals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breast" title="Breast">breasts</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Buttocks" title="Buttocks">buttocks</a> are visible could be considered <a href="/wiki/Indecent_exposure" title="Indecent exposure">indecent exposure</a>. Pubic area or genital coverage is the most frequently encountered minimum found cross-culturally and regardless of climate, implying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_convention" title="Social convention">social convention</a> as the basis of customs. Clothing also may be used to communicate social status, wealth, group identity, and individualism.
</p><p>Some forms of <a href="/wiki/Personal_protective_equipment" title="Personal protective equipment">personal protective equipment</a> amount to clothing, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coveralls" title="Coveralls">coveralls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaps" title="Chaps">chaps</a> or a doctor's <a href="/wiki/White_coat" title="White coat">white coat</a>, with similar requirements for maintenance and <a href="/wiki/Laundry" title="Laundry">cleaning</a> as other textiles (<a href="/wiki/Boxing_glove" title="Boxing glove">boxing gloves</a> function both as protective equipment and as a <a href="/wiki/Sparring" title="Sparring">sparring</a> <a href="/wiki/Weapon" title="Weapon">weapon</a>, so the equipment aspect rises above the glove aspect). More specialized forms of protective equipment, such as <a href="/wiki/Face_shield" title="Face shield">face shields</a> are classified protective accessories. At the far extreme, self-enclosing <a href="/wiki/Diving_suit" title="Diving suit">diving suits</a> or <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">space suits</a> are <a href="/wiki/Form-fitting_garment" title="Form-fitting garment">form fitting</a> body covers, and amount to a form of dress, without being clothing per se, while containing enough <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_technology" title="High technology">high technology</a> to amount to more of a <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tool</a> than a garment. This line will continue to blur as <a href="/wiki/Wearable_technology" title="Wearable technology">wearable technology</a> embeds assistive devices directly into the fabric itself; the enabling innovations are ultra <a href="/wiki/Low-power_electronics" title="Low-power electronics">low power consumption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flexible_electronics" title="Flexible electronics">flexible electronic substrates</a>.
</p><p>Clothing also hybridizes into a personal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">transportation</a> system (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_skates" title="Ice skates">ice skates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roller_skates" title="Roller skates">roller skates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cargo_pants" title="Cargo pants">cargo pants</a>, other outdoor <a href="/wiki/Survival_kit" title="Survival kit">survival gear</a>, <a href="/wiki/One-man_band" title="One-man band">one-man band</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Concealment_device" title="Concealment device">concealment</a> system (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stage_magician" title="Stage magician">stage magicians</a>, hidden linings or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pockets" title="Pockets">pockets</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tradecraft" title="Tradecraft">tradecraft</a>, integrated holsters for <a href="/wiki/Concealed_carry" title="Concealed carry">concealed carry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchandise" title="Merchandise">merchandise</a>-laden <a href="/wiki/Trench_coat" title="Trench coat">trench coats</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Black_market" title="Black market">black market</a> — where the purpose of the clothing often carries over into <a href="/wiki/Disguise" title="Disguise">disguise</a>). A mode of dress fit to purpose, whether stylistic or functional, is known as an outfit or ensemble.
</p>

style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="display:block; text-align:center;"><b><a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae"><br/>H<br/><br/>o<br/><br/>m<br/><br/>i<br/><br/>n<br/><br/>i<br/><br/>d<br/><br/>s</a></b></span></div></div></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" id="Caption" style="padding:0;margin:0 0.2em 0.7em 0.2em"><div class="toccolours" id="Caption" style="margin:0 0.5em;border-style:none;clear:both;text-align:center;width:26.8em"><div style="float:left;font-size:85%;">(<a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a>)</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_clothing_and_textiles" title="History of clothing and textiles">History of clothing and textiles</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_fashion" title="History of Western fashion">History of Western fashion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Category:History_of_clothing" title="Category:History of clothing">Category:History of clothing</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolution_of_hair" title="Evolution of hair">Evolution of hair</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_use">Early use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scientists have never agreed on when humans began wearing clothes and estimates suggested by various experts have ranged greatly, from 40,000 to as many as 3 million years ago. 
</p><p>Recent studies by Ralf Kittler, Manfred Kayser and Mark Stoneking—<a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologists</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_for_Evolutionary_Anthropology" title="Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology">Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology</a>—have attempted to constrain the most recent date of the introduction of clothing with an indirect method relying on <a href="/wiki/Louse" title="Louse">lice</a>. The rationale for this method of dating stems from the fact that the human body louse cannot live outside of clothing, dying after only a few hours without shelter. This strongly implies that the date of the body louse's speciation from its parent, <i><a href="/wiki/Pediculus_humanus" title="Pediculus humanus">Pediculus humanus</a>,</i> can have taken place no earlier than the earliest human adoption of clothing. This date, at which the body louse (<i><a href="/wiki/Body_louse" title="Body louse">P. humanus corporus</a></i>) diverged from both its parent species and its sibling subspecies, the head louse (<i><a href="/wiki/Head_louse" title="Head louse">P. humanus capitus</a></i>), can be determined by the number of mutations each has developed during the intervening time. Such mutations occur at a known rate and the date of last-common-ancestor for two species can therefore be estimated from their frequency. These studies have produced dates from 40,000 to 170,000 years ago, with a greatest likelihood of speciation lying at about 107,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kittler, Kayser and Stoneking suggest that the invention of clothing may have coincided with the northward migration of modern <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> away from the warm <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, which is thought to have begun between 100,000 and 50,000 years ago. A second group of researchers, also relying on the genetic clock, estimate that clothing originated between 30,000 and 114,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dating with direct archeological evidence produces dates consistent with those hinted at by lice. In September 2021, scientists reported evidence of clothes being made 120,000 years ago based on findings in deposits in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iSC-20210916_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-iSC-20210916-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TG-20210916_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-TG-20210916-4">[4]</a></sup> However, despite these indications, there is no single estimate that is widely accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoneking_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stoneking-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to anthropologists and archaeologists, the earliest clothing likely consisted of <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">fur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>, leaves, or grass that was draped, wrapped, or tied around the body. Knowledge of such clothing remains inferential, as <a href="/wiki/Clothing_material" title="Clothing material">clothing materials</a> deteriorate quickly compared with stone, bone, shell, and metal artifacts. Archeologists have identified very early <a href="/wiki/Sewing_needle" title="Sewing needle">sewing needles</a> of bone and ivory from about 30,000 BC, found near <a href="/wiki/Kostenki" title="Kostenki">Kostenki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Dyed <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> fibers that could have been used in clothing have been found in a prehistoric cave in the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Republic of Georgia</a> that date back to 34,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Making_clothing">Making clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Making clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garment_industry" title="Garment industry">Garment industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knitting" title="Knitting">knitting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma,_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg"><img alt="Sari" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="705" data-file-width="498" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg/220px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg/330px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg/440px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma,_Lady_Going_for_Pooja.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> lady wearing <a href="/wiki/Sari" title="Sari">sari</a>, one of the most ancient and popular pieces of clothing in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Raja_Ravi_Varma" title="Raja Ravi Varma">Raja Ravi Varma</a></div></div></div>
<p>Some human cultures, such as the various peoples of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, traditionally make their clothing entirely of prepared and decorated furs and skins. Other cultures supplemented or replaced leather and skins with cloth: woven, knitted, or twined from various animal and vegetable fibers including wool, <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, hemp, and <a href="/wiki/Ramie" title="Ramie">ramie</a>.
</p><p>Although modern consumers may take the production of clothing for granted, making fabric by hand is a tedious and labor-intensive process involving fiber making, spinning, and weaving. The <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile</a> industry was the first to be mechanized – with the <a href="/wiki/Power_loom" title="Power loom">powered loom</a> – during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.
</p><p>Different cultures have evolved various ways of creating clothes out of cloth. One approach simply involves draping the cloth. Many people wore, and still wear, garments consisting of rectangles of cloth wrapped to fit – for example, the <a href="/wiki/Dhoti" title="Dhoti">dhoti</a> for men and the <a href="/wiki/Sari" title="Sari">sari</a> for women in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, the Scottish <a href="/wiki/Kilt" title="Kilt">kilt</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_(island)" title="Java (island)">Javanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Sarong" title="Sarong">sarong</a>. The clothes may simply be tied up (dhoti and sari) or implement pins or belts to hold the garments in place (kilt and sarong). The cloth remains uncut, and people of various <a href="/wiki/Clothing_sizes" title="Clothing sizes">sizes</a> can wear the garment.
</p><p>Another approach involves measuring, cutting, and sewing the cloth by hand or with a <a href="/wiki/Sewing_machine" title="Sewing machine">sewing machine</a>. Clothing can be cut from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sewing_pattern" title="Sewing pattern">sewing pattern</a> and adjusted by a tailor to the wearer's measurements. An adjustable sewing mannequin or <a href="/wiki/Dress_form" title="Dress form">dress form</a> is used to create form-fitting clothing. If the fabric is expensive, the tailor tries to use every bit of the cloth rectangle in constructing the clothing; perhaps cutting triangular pieces from one corner of the cloth, and adding them elsewhere as <a href="/wiki/Gusset" title="Gusset">gussets</a>. Traditional European patterns for <a href="/wiki/Shirt" title="Shirt">shirts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemise" title="Chemise">chemises</a> take this approach. These remnants can also be reused to make patchwork pockets, hats, vests, and <a href="/wiki/Skirt" title="Skirt">skirts</a>.
</p><p>Modern European <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">fashion</a> treats cloth much less conservatively, typically cutting in such a way as to leave various odd-shaped cloth remnants. Industrial sewing operations sell these as waste; domestic sewers may turn them into <a href="/wiki/Quilt" title="Quilt">quilts</a>.
</p><p>In the thousands of years that humans have been making clothing, they have created an astonishing array of styles, many of which have been reconstructed from surviving garments, <a href="/wiki/Photograph" title="Photograph">photographs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">paintings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a>, etc., as well as from written descriptions. Costume history can inspire current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fashion_designer" title="Fashion designer">fashion designers</a>, as well as costumiers for <a href="/wiki/Play_(theatre)" title="Play (theatre)">plays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">films</a>, <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Historical_reenactment" title="Historical reenactment">historical reenactment</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing_as_comfort">Clothing as comfort</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Clothing as comfort">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1201" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg/220px-Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg/330px-Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg/440px-Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sp5hnk_oh0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young woman wearing <a href="/wiki/T-shirt" title="T-shirt">t-shirt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shorts" title="Shorts">shorts</a> at the warm summer in <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land" title="Åland">Åland</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Comfort" title="Comfort">Comfort</a> is related to various perceptions, physiological, social, and psychological needs, and after food, it is clothing that satisfies these comfort needs. Clothing provides aesthetic, tactile, thermal, moisture, and pressure comfort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Aesthetic comfort: Visual perception is influenced by <a href="/wiki/Color_of_clothing" title="Color of clothing">color</a>, fabric construction, style, garment fit, fashion compatibility, and finish of clothing material. Aesthetic comfort is necessary for psychological and social comfort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation_in_humans" title="Thermoregulation in humans">Thermoregulation in humans</a> and thermophysiological comfort: Thermophysiological comfort is the capacity of the clothing material that makes the balance of moisture and heat between the body and the environment. It is a property of textile materials that creates ease by maintaining moisture and thermal levels in a human's resting and active states. The selection of textile material significantly affects the comfort of the wearer. Different textile fibers have unique properties that make them suitable for use in various environments. Natural fibers are breathable and absorb moisture, and synthetic fibers are hydrophobic; they repel moisture and do not allow air to pass. Different environments demand a diverse selection of clothing materials. Hence, the appropriate choice is  important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The major determinants that influence thermophysiological comfort are permeable construction, heat, and moisture transfer rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
<ul><li>Thermal comfort: One primary criterion for our physiological needs is thermal comfort. The heat dissipation effectiveness of clothing gives the wearer a neither very hot nor very cold feel. The optimum temperature for thermal comfort of the skin surface is between 28 and 30 degrees Celsius, i.e., a neutral temperature. Thermophysiology reacts whenever the temperature falls below or exceeds the neutral point on either side; it is discomforting below 28 and above 30 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Clothing maintains a thermal balance; it keeps the skin dry and cool. It helps to keep the body from overheating while avoiding heat from the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:022_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:022-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li>Moisture comfort: Moisture comfort is the prevention of a damp sensation. According to Hollies' research, it feels uncomfortable when more than "50% to 65% of the body is wet."</li></ul></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Touch" title="Touch">Tactile</a> comfort: Tactile comfort is a resistance to the discomfort related to the friction created by clothing against the body. It is related to the smoothness, roughness, softness, and stiffness of the fabric used in clothing. The degree of tactile discomfort may vary between individuals, which is possible due to various factors including allergies, tickling, prickling, skin abrasion, coolness, and the fabric's weight, structure, and thickness. There are specific surface finishes (mechanical and chemical) that can enhance tactile comfort. Fleece sweatshirts and velvet clothing, for example. Soft, clingy, stiff, heavy, light, hard, sticky, scratchy, prickly are all terms used to describe tactile sensations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li>
<li>Pressure comfort: The comfort of the human body's pressure receptors' (present in the skin) sensory response towards clothing. Fabric with lycra feels more comfortable because of this response and superior pressure comfort. The sensation response is influenced by the material's structure: snugging, looseness, heavy, light, soft, or stiff structuring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions">Functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Well-clothed_baby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1989" data-file-width="1989" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Well-clothed_baby.jpg/170px-Well-clothed_baby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Well-clothed_baby.jpg/255px-Well-clothed_baby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Well-clothed_baby.jpg/340px-Well-clothed_baby.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Well-clothed_baby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">baby</a> wearing many items of <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a> clothing: <a href="/wiki/Headband" title="Headband">headband</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cap" title="Cap">cap</a>, fur-lined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coat_(clothing)" title="Coat (clothing)">coat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scarf" title="Scarf">scarf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sweater" title="Sweater">sweater</a></div></div></div>

video on social expression through dress</div></div></div>
<p>The most obvious function of clothing is to protect the wearer from the elements. It serves to prevent <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> damage and provides protection from <a href="/wiki/Sunburn" title="Sunburn">sunburn</a>. In the cold, it offers <a href="/wiki/Thermal_insulation" title="Thermal insulation">thermal insulation</a>. The shelter can reduce the functional need for clothing. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coat_(clothing)" title="Coat (clothing)">coats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hat" title="Hat">hats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glove" title="Glove">gloves</a>, and other outer layers are normally removed when entering a warm place. Similarly, clothing has seasonal and regional aspects so that thinner materials and fewer layers of clothing generally are worn in warmer regions and seasons than in colder ones. Boots, hats, jackets, ponchos, and coats designed to protect from rain and snow are specialized clothing items.
</p><p>Clothing has been made from a wide variety of materials, ranging from leather and <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">furs</a> to woven fabrics to elaborate and exotic natural and <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fiber" title="Synthetic fiber">synthetic fabrics</a>. Not all body coverings are regarded as clothing. Articles carried rather than worn normally are considered <a href="/wiki/Fashion_accessory" title="Fashion accessory">accessories</a> rather than clothing (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handbags" title="Handbags">Handbags</a>), items worn on a single part of the body and easily removed (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scarves" title="Scarves">scarves</a>), worn purely for adornment (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewelry" title="Jewelry">jewelry</a>), or items that do not serve a protective function. For instance, corrective <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyeglasses" title="Eyeglasses">eyeglasses</a>, Arctic <a href="/wiki/Snow_goggles" title="Snow goggles">goggles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sunglasses" title="Sunglasses">sunglasses</a> would not be considered an accessory because of their protective functions.
</p><p>Clothing protects against many things that might injure or irritate the naked human body, including rain, snow, wind, and other weather, as well as from the sun. Garments that are too sheer, thin, small, or tight offer less protection. Appropriate clothes can also reduce risk during activities such as work or sport. Some clothing protects from specific hazards, such as <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, toxic chemicals, weather, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armor" title="Armor">weapons</a>, and contact with abrasive substances.
</p><p>Humans have devised clothing solutions to environmental or other hazards: such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_suits" title="Space suits">space suits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioned_clothing" title="Air conditioned clothing">air conditioned clothing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armor" title="Armor">armor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diving_suits" title="Diving suits">diving suits</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swimsuits" title="Swimsuits">swimsuits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping#Protective_clothing" title="Beekeeping">bee-keeper gear</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motorcycle_leathers" title="Motorcycle leathers">motorcycle leathers</a>, <a href="/wiki/High-visibility_clothing" title="High-visibility clothing">high-visibility clothing</a>, and other pieces of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protective_clothing" title="Protective clothing">protective clothing</a>. The distinction between clothing and protective equipment is not always clear-cut since clothes designed to be fashionable often have protective value, and clothes designed for function often incorporate <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">fashion</a> in their design. The choice of clothes also has social implications. They cover parts of the body that social norms require to be covered, act as a form of adornment, and serve other social purposes. Someone who lacks the means to procure appropriate clothing due to poverty or affordability, or simply lack of inclination, sometimes is said to be worn, ragged, or shabby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clothing performs a range of social and <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> functions, such as individual, occupational and gender differentiation, and social status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flugel1930_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flugel1930-34">[34]</a></sup> In many societies, norms about clothing reflect standards of <a href="/wiki/Modesty" title="Modesty">modesty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">social status</a>. Clothing may also function as adornment and an expression of personal taste or style.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarship">Scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Function_of_clothing">Function of clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Function of clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="746" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg/220px-The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg/330px-The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg/440px-The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Real_and_Its_Ideal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Real and Its Ideal", 1898 illustration by <a href="/wiki/E._J._Sullivan" title="E. J. Sullivan">E. J. Sullivan</a> for <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Sartor_Resartus" title="Sartor Resartus">Sartor Resartus</a></i> (1833–34)</div></div></div>
<p>Serious books on clothing and its functions appear from the nineteenth century as <a href="/wiki/History_of_colonialism" title="History of colonialism">European colonial powers</a> interacted with new environments such as tropical ones in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffreys1858_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffreys1858-35">[35]</a></sup> Some scientific research into the multiple functions of clothing in the first half of the twentieth century, with publications such as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=J.C._Fl%C3%BCgel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="J.C. Flügel (page does not exist)">J.C. Flügel</a>'s <i>Psychology of Clothes</i> in 1930,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flugel1930_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flugel1930-34">[34]</a></sup> and Newburgh's seminal <i>Physiology of Heat Regulation and The Science of Clothing</i> in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newburgh1968_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newburgh1968-36">[36]</a></sup> By 1968, the field of environmental physiology had advanced and expanded significantly, but the science of clothing in relation to environmental physiology had changed little.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hertig1969_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hertig1969-37">[37]</a></sup> There has since been considerable research, and the knowledge base has grown significantly, but the main concepts remain unchanged, and indeed, Newburgh's book continues to be cited by contemporary authors, including those attempting to develop thermoregulatory models of clothing development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilligan2010_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilligan2010-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (February 2019)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_clothing">History of clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History of clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Clothing reveals much about human history. According to Professor Kiki Smith of Smith College, garments preserved in collections are resources for study similar to books and paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Should_These_Clothes_Be_Saved_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Should_These_Clothes_Be_Saved-39">[39]</a></sup> Scholars around the world have studied a wide range of clothing topics, including the history of specific items of clothing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> clothing styles in different cultural groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and the business of clothing and fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The textile curator Linda Baumgarten writes that "clothing provides a remarkable picture of the daily lives, beliefs, expectations, and hopes of those who lived in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clothing presents a number of challenges to historians. Clothing made of textiles or skins is subject to decay, and the erosion of physical integrity may be seen as a loss of cultural information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Costume collections often focus on important pieces of clothing considered unique or otherwise significant, limiting the opportunities scholars have to study everyday clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Should_These_Clothes_Be_Saved_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Should_These_Clothes_Be_Saved-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_aspects">Cultural aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cultural aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_differentiation">Gender differentiation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Gender differentiation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>In most cultures, gender differentiation of clothing is considered appropriate. The differences are in styles, colors, fabrics, and types.
</p><p>In contemporary Western societies, <a href="/wiki/Skirt" title="Skirt">skirts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dress_(garment)" title="Dress (garment)">dresses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/High-heeled_shoe" title="High-heeled shoe">high-heeled shoes</a> are usually seen as women's clothing, while <a href="/wiki/Necktie" title="Necktie">neckties</a> usually are seen as men's clothing. <a href="/wiki/Trousers" title="Trousers">Trousers</a> were once seen as exclusively men's clothing, but nowadays are worn by both genders. Men's clothes are often more practical (that is, they can function well under a wide variety of situations), but a wider range of clothing styles is available for women. Typically, men are allowed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bare_chested" title="Bare chested">bare their chests</a> in a greater variety of public places. It is generally common for a woman to wear clothing perceived as masculine, while the opposite is seen as unusual. Contemporary men may sometimes choose to wear <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_skirts" title="Men's skirts">men's skirts</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Togas" title="Togas">togas</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kilt" title="Kilt">kilts</a> in particular cultures, especially on ceremonial occasions. In previous times, such garments often were worn as normal daily clothing by men.
</p><p>In some cultures, <a href="/wiki/Sumptuary_law" title="Sumptuary law">sumptuary laws</a> regulate what men and women are required to wear. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> requires women to wear certain forms of attire, usually <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a>. What items required varies in different Muslim societies; however, women are usually required to cover more of their bodies than men. Articles of clothing Muslim women wear under these laws or traditions range from the <a href="/wiki/Headscarf" title="Headscarf">head-scarf</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Burqa" title="Burqa">burqa</a>.
</p><p>Some contemporary clothing styles designed to be worn by either gender, such as T-shirts, have started out as menswear, but some articles, such as the <a href="/wiki/Fedora" title="Fedora">fedora</a>, originally were a style for women.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_status">Social status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Social status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:42px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rajput_Sherwani_2014-04-23_04-27.JPG" title="Achkan sherwani and churidar (lower body) worn by Arvind Singh Mewar and his kin during a Hindu wedding in Rajasthan, India, are items traditionally worn by the elites of the Indian subcontinent"><img alt="Achkan sherwani and churidar (lower body) worn by Arvind Singh Mewar and his kin during a Hindu wedding in Rajasthan, India, are items traditionally worn by the elites of the Indian subcontinent" data-file-height="1160" data-file-width="1594" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rajput_Sherwani_2014-04-23_04-27.JPG/200px-Rajput_Sherwani_2014-04-23_04-27.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rajput_Sherwani_2014-04-23_04-27.JPG/300px-Rajput_Sherwani_2014-04-23_04-27.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Rajput_Sherwani_2014-04-23_04-27.JPG/400px-Rajput_Sherwani_2014-04-23_04-27.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MyBarong_created_this_Custom_tailored_Barong_Tagalog_for_my_wedding.jpg" title="A Barong Tagalog made for a wedding ceremony"><img alt="A Barong Tagalog made for a wedding ceremony" data-file-height="3300" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/MyBarong_created_this_Custom_tailored_Barong_Tagalog_for_my_wedding.jpg/145px-MyBarong_created_this_Custom_tailored_Barong_Tagalog_for_my_wedding.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/MyBarong_created_this_Custom_tailored_Barong_Tagalog_for_my_wedding.jpg/218px-MyBarong_created_this_Custom_tailored_Barong_Tagalog_for_my_wedding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/MyBarong_created_this_Custom_tailored_Barong_Tagalog_for_my_wedding.jpg/291px-MyBarong_created_this_Custom_tailored_Barong_Tagalog_for_my_wedding.jpg 2x" width="145"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barong_Tagalog" title="Barong Tagalog">Barong Tagalog</a> made for a wedding ceremony
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:28.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prokudin-Gorskii-19.jpg" title="Alim Khan's bemedaled robe conveys a social message about his wealth, status, and power"><img alt="Alim Khan's bemedaled robe conveys a social message about his wealth, status, and power" data-file-height="2859" data-file-width="3307" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Prokudin-Gorskii-19.jpg/200px-Prokudin-Gorskii-19.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Prokudin-Gorskii-19.jpg/300px-Prokudin-Gorskii-19.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Prokudin-Gorskii-19.jpg/400px-Prokudin-Gorskii-19.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammed_Alim_Khan" title="Mohammed Alim Khan">Alim Khan</a>'s bemedaled <a href="/wiki/Robe" title="Robe">robe</a> conveys a social message about his wealth, status, and power
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In some societies, clothing may be used to indicate rank or <a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">status</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, for example, only senators could wear garments dyed with <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">Tyrian purple</a>. In traditional <a href="/wiki/Native_Hawaiians" title="Native Hawaiians">Hawaiian</a> society, only high-ranking chiefs could wear <a href="/wiki/Feather_cloak" title="Feather cloak">feather cloaks</a> and palaoa, or carved <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whale</a> teeth. In China, before establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">republic</a>, only the emperor could wear <a href="/wiki/Yellow" title="Yellow">yellow</a>. History provides many examples of elaborate <a href="/wiki/Sumptuary_law" title="Sumptuary law">sumptuary laws</a> that regulated what people could wear. In societies without such laws, which includes most modern societies, social status is signaled by the purchase of rare or luxury items that are limited by cost to those with wealth or status. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">peer pressure</a> influences clothing choice.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clothing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_clothing" title="Religious clothing">Religious clothing</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg" title="The Buddha wearing kāṣāya robes, originating from ancient India, these robes were worn by fully ordained Buddhist monks and nuns"><img alt="The Buddha wearing kāṣāya robes, originating from ancient India, these robes were worn by fully ordained Buddhist monks and nuns" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="1746" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/120px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/181px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/241px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a> wearing <a href="/wiki/Kasaya_(clothing)" title="Kasaya (clothing)">kāṣāya</a> robes, originating from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_India" title="Ancient India">ancient India</a>, these robes were worn by fully ordained Buddhist monks and nuns
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clerical_clothing.jpg" title="Clerical clothing worn by Catholic priests"><img alt="Clerical clothing worn by Catholic priests" data-file-height="1916" data-file-width="1634" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Clerical_clothing.jpg/170px-Clerical_clothing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Clerical_clothing.jpg/256px-Clerical_clothing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Clerical_clothing.jpg/341px-Clerical_clothing.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Clerical_clothing" title="Clerical clothing">Clerical clothing</a> worn by <a href="/wiki/Clergy#Christianity" title="Clergy">Catholic priests</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Some religious clothing might be considered a special case of occupational clothing. Sometimes it is worn only during the performance of religious ceremonies. However, it also may be worn every day as a marker for special religious status.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Sikhs wear a turban as it is a part of their religion.
</p><p>In some religions such as <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>
the cleanliness of religious dresses is of paramount importance and is considered to indicate purity. Jewish ritual requires rending of one's upper garment as a sign of mourning.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (February 2019)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Quran says about husbands and wives, regarding clothing: "...They are clothing/covering (Libaas) for you; and you for them" (chapter 2:187).<a href="/wiki/Clergy#Christianity" title="Clergy">Christian clergy</a> members wear religious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestments" title="Vestments">vestments</a> during <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical</a> services and may wear specific <a href="/wiki/Clerical_clothing" title="Clerical clothing">non-liturgical clothing</a> at other times.
</p><p>Clothing appears in numerous contexts in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>. The most prominent passages are: the story of <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">Adam and Eve</a> who made coverings for themselves out of <a href="/wiki/Fig_leaf" title="Fig leaf">fig leaves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_(son_of_Jacob)" title="Joseph (son of Jacob)">Joseph</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_many_colors" title="Coat of many colors">coat of many colors</a>, and the clothing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judah_(Bible)" title="Judah (Bible)">Judah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tamar_(Genesis)" title="Tamar (Genesis)">Tamar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mordecai" title="Mordecai">Mordecai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Esther" title="Esther">Esther</a>. Furthermore, the priests officiating in the Temple in Jerusalem had very specific garments, the lack of which made one liable to death.
</p>
